    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6005150746341651676">Confessions of a Crap Artist is one of Philip K. Dick's weirdest and most accomplished novels. Jack Isidore is a crap artist -- a collector of crackpot ideas (among other things, he believes that the earth is hollow and that sunlight has weight) and worthless objects, a man so grossly unequipped for real life that his sister and brother-in-law feel compelled to rescue him </span>
  <span id="freeText6005150746341651676" style="display:none">Confessions of a Crap Artist is one of Philip K. Dick's weirdest and most accomplished novels. Jack Isidore is a crap artist -- a collector of crackpot ideas (among other things, he believes that the earth is hollow and that sunlight has weight) and worthless objects, a man so grossly unequipped for real life that his sister and brother-in-law feel compelled to rescue him from it. But seen through Jack's murderously innocent gaze, Charlie and Juddy Hume prove to be just as sealed off from reality, in thrall to obsessions that are slightly more acceptable than Jack's, but a great deal uglier.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6005150746341651676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_486539561" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/486539561" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/486539561?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer486539561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1570379044789070926">Confessions of a Crap Artist by Philip K. Dick is not a normal, wildly imaginative science fiction offering from one of the most accomplished, innovative and influential science fiction writers of the modern era. It is instead a novel of complicated interpersonal family dynamics. But it is Dick’s voice, his resonate, edgy and unorthodox observant style that lends the book its greater depth. <br/><br/>The reader is frequently reminded of PKD’s penchant for the unusual, and this dicey undertone is what prev</span>
  <span id="freeText1570379044789070926" style="display:none">Confessions of a Crap Artist by Philip K. Dick is not a normal, wildly imaginative science fiction offering from one of the most accomplished, innovative and influential science fiction writers of the modern era. It is instead a novel of complicated interpersonal family dynamics. But it is Dick’s voice, his resonate, edgy and unorthodox observant style that lends the book its greater depth. <br><br>The reader is frequently reminded of PKD’s penchant for the unusual, and this dicey undertone is what prevents this from becoming an interesting but otherwise pedestrian suburban melodrama. It is the shadows and penumbra of PKD’s schizophrenia that spice this family drama set in Marin County, California in the late 1950s, giving it a delicious and hypnotic tone, like wild Patsy Cline singing a heartfelt ballad, it is the underlying vibrance that makes it great. <br><br>Dick’s talent for characterization is in full bloom, the character of Fay Hume, perhaps modeled after Hemingway’s Lady Britt, rivals <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/636108.Dr__Bloodmoney" title="Dr. Bloodmoney by Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">Dr. Bloodmoney</a>’s Hoppy Harrington as Dick’s most intriguing character. Ultimately this is also an indictment, in Dick’s unique presentation, of middle class bourgeois suburban values in the 1950s.<br><br>What else makes this a great read for a reader in 2013 is the Steinbeck-like glimpse of 1950s life in Northern California. This is a time capsule, a surreal glimpse at another age, of my grandparents’ peers in the fullness of life, a generation that younger readers may not know at all.<br><br>Reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22587.Divine_Invasions_A_Life_of_Philip_K__Dick" title="Divine Invasions A Life of Philip K. Dick by Lawrence Sutin" rel="nofollow noopener">Divine Invasions: A Life of Philip K. Dick</a>, Lawrence Sutin's well researched biography of Dick makes this even more enjoyable and enlightening because we can see the autobiographical influences from his experiences in northern California in the late 50s and early 60s. This also makes understandable Dick's early intention to write more mainstream books than science fiction as he was certainly capable.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1507077024i/24079890.jpg" width="300" height="196" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="1570379044789070926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer621936836424533188"><i>"Science in baffled by the unreason of the hoi polloi. The moods of the mass can't be fathomed, that's a fact."</i><br/>- Philip K. Dick, Confessions of a Crap Artist<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525585690i/25489261._SY540_.jpg' width='400' height='580' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Jack Isadore is a bit a lune. He believes in crack-pot theories about the end of the world, has funky obscessions and ticks. He's a couple nuts short of being a fruit cake. Eventually, he ends up living with his rich sister and her husband. With them, Jack discovers he isn't the only crazy one. It seems most people, even those who seem to ha</span>
  <span id="freeText621936836424533188" style="display:none"><i>"Science in baffled by the unreason of the hoi polloi. The moods of the mass can't be fathomed, that's a fact."</i><br>- Philip K. Dick, Confessions of a Crap Artist<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525585690i/25489261._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="580" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Jack Isadore is a bit a lune. He believes in crack-pot theories about the end of the world, has funky obscessions and ticks. He's a couple nuts short of being a fruit cake. Eventually, he ends up living with his rich sister and her husband. With them, Jack discovers he isn't the only crazy one. It seems most people, even those who seem to have "everything" and fit into our reality better, are only a couple inches away from the void.<br><br>I went into this book blind. I've read a bunch of other Philip K. Dick novels, but never realized THIS is his <s>only</s> [one of his few]* non-scifi novel[s]. It shares more with Raymond Carver and Jonathan Franzen than it does with Vernor Vinge and Kurt Vonnegut. I liked it, but it was a bit tedious in parts. Dick's ability to capture characters is on point in this book. All the major characters are amazing, especially Jack's sister Fayy Hume and her husband Charley. Wow. I didn't like them, but after finishing this book I felt like I was RELATED to them.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer100084937" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer256968828262881253">I've never read a book about so many irredeemable assholes before. There really isn't a single character anyone in their right mind would care about here. Children, maybe, but PKD pretty much completely ignores them, milking the assholes instead for all they are worth. It got really hard to read towards the middle, not so much because it was tedious or badly written, but because I wanted to rip the book to shreds every couple of minutes after something some asshole said or thought. I can't imagi</span>
  <span id="freeText256968828262881253" style="display:none">I've never read a book about so many irredeemable assholes before. There really isn't a single character anyone in their right mind would care about here. Children, maybe, but PKD pretty much completely ignores them, milking the assholes instead for all they are worth. It got really hard to read towards the middle, not so much because it was tedious or badly written, but because I wanted to rip the book to shreds every couple of minutes after something some asshole said or thought. I can't imagine how hard it must have been to write. I think PKD could have finished it with an epilogue to the effect of: "And this is why I write science fiction. Because when I think of real people I think of people like these."<br /><br />I gave it three stars for having the sense to end quickly, for producing very strong emotions in me, and for channeling Yates in places. </span>

<div id="review_1437776586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sean Blake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25471005-sean-blake"><img alt="Sean Blake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528978476p2/25471005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Blake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Blake" href="/user/show/25471005-sean-blake">Sean Blake</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575074644" href="/book/show/628681.Confessions_of_a_Crap_Artist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25471005-sean-blake?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1437776586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1698127124358664564"><b>We are all crap artists</b><br/><br/><i>Confessions of a Crap Artist</i> is fantastically well-written novel by Philip K. Dick. A novel of two halves, one of a small-town Californian domestic drama, the other an analysis of post-Roswell paranoia in America, it contains some of Philip K. Dick's best writing. Despite not being one of his popular science fiction tales, this realist novel contains all the philosophical depth, pondering and Freudian insight found in his most popular books.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2702888165" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2702888165" />
    <a title="Jimmy Ele" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30541335-jimmy-ele"><img alt="Jimmy Ele" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436125044p2/30541335.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2702888165?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jimmy Ele" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jimmy Ele" href="/user/show/30541335-jimmy-ele">Jimmy Ele</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2702888165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3863167797964586022">This is an underrated gem. P.K.D. writing from the different perspectives of all the characters in the story is insightful, in that it gives us access into the subtleties of human perception. The story gets treated from varying perspectives and so it gives us the story from several angles. All of the judgments that the characters make about themselves and others are indicative of the judgmental and reasoning factors inherent in the minds of all human beings. <br/><br/>We tend to judge ourselves and others</span>
  <span id="freeText3863167797964586022" style="display:none">This is an underrated gem. P.K.D. writing from the different perspectives of all the characters in the story is insightful, in that it gives us access into the subtleties of human perception. The story gets treated from varying perspectives and so it gives us the story from several angles. All of the judgments that the characters make about themselves and others are indicative of the judgmental and reasoning factors inherent in the minds of all human beings. <br /><br />We tend to judge ourselves and others constantly and are always reasoning about our own choices in life. Some of the characters that are viewed as nut-jobs in the story by certain characters get to tell the story from their own perspectives. And in the end it becomes exposed that all of the characters (no matter how socially adjusted they are) carry deep psychological scarring and are in need of psychiatric help.<br /><br />The most outwardly seeming socially well adjusted rational characters end up making the biggest life threatening and life altering decisions for most of the characters involved. The characters in the story that are considered crazy by the rational ones end up being the least harmful to everyone involved. <br /><br />P.K.D. is a master writer when it comes to human perception and this is definitely a testament to his analytical and writing powers. <br /><br />I highly recommend for anyone who is attempting to view the world through several different lenses of perception.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3863167797964586022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/8138700?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Logan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Logan" href="/user/show/379310-logan">Logan</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8138700" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3307601415088819620">This is not a sci-fi novel, despite the hip, design-centric, adroid-esque cover. That cover is complete fucking bullshit.<br/><br/>Anyway, this one's more of a relationship/ family drama. I felt that it was readily apparent in this book that its author was trying to work things out. I don't know if that means it was heavy-handed, or if I've been influenced by what I've heard about PK Dick, but that's that. <br/><br/>There's lots of introspection, and false epiphanies, as well as real epiphanies, and a lot of philos</span>
  <span id="freeText3307601415088819620" style="display:none">This is not a sci-fi novel, despite the hip, design-centric, adroid-esque cover. That cover is complete fucking bullshit.<br /><br />Anyway, this one's more of a relationship/ family drama. I felt that it was readily apparent in this book that its author was trying to work things out. I don't know if that means it was heavy-handed, or if I've been influenced by what I've heard about PK Dick, but that's that. <br /><br />There's lots of introspection, and false epiphanies, as well as real epiphanies, and a lot of philosphical or quasi-philospical meanderings about why we do the things we do and how we end up where we are, what quantifies success, what is normal, what is crazy, and what exactly are we responsible for, and how much is it worth. Really, I do think this is a good thing. But sometimes the story suffered for it because I think sometimes these meanderings, these attempts to figure out the puzzle, forced the story in directions not completely natural.<br /><br />But perhaps the questions posed are more important than the story they came from? <br /><br />I would give this book 4 stars, but the last 60 pages or so were kind of hard to get thru. The last line of the book is pretty fuckin' great though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3307601415088819620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_448847973" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/448847973" />
    <a title="Ryandake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4057623-ryandake"><img alt="Ryandake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280111549p2/4057623.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/448847973?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryandake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryandake" href="/user/show/4057623-ryandake">Ryandake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer448847973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11755685928775285266">wow, a difficult book to assess.<br/><br/>you wouldn't think there'd be <i>that</i> much difference between how people thought in the 1950s and now. i mean, it's not like it was the Middle Ages or something.<br/><br/>but it might as well have been, in so many respects.<br/><br/>since you can read the summary above, i won't bore you with it. instead, i'll tell you what's difficult (and also well-done) about this novel.<br/><br/>first off, as a feminist, the misogyny is like a bullhorn in the face. you can't escape it--even the female characte</span>
  <span id="freeText11755685928775285266" style="display:none">wow, a difficult book to assess.<br /><br />you wouldn't think there'd be <i>that</i> much difference between how people thought in the 1950s and now. i mean, it's not like it was the Middle Ages or something.<br /><br />but it might as well have been, in so many respects.<br /><br />since you can read the summary above, i won't bore you with it. instead, i'll tell you what's difficult (and also well-done) about this novel.<br /><br />first off, as a feminist, the misogyny is like a bullhorn in the face. you can't escape it--even the female characters hate themselves in some particularly corrosive ways. the main female character, fay, is a puzzle for a contemporary reader. the other characters clearly want you to see her as merely a manipulative, possibly psychopathic (their diagnosis, not mine) narcissist. (all reported in the most 1950s freudian-analyst terms, which, while sometimes delightfully retro, do make one wish for a long, hot shower after.) <br /><br />for me this is the central problem of this book: how to see fay? if i accept the 1950s limitations of what women were supposed to be, then perhaps yes, she is manipulative and uncaring. but how else can the powerless be, except manipulative? and who in her life (besides her children) is there really to care for, that cares for her?<br /><br />so fay is a problem.<br /><br />her husband, charley, is an interesting sort--he holds all the cards, and yet has convinced himself that he's been victimized by his wife. his thought processes are quite icky but in their way, almost convincing. one could almost agree with him at some points. now <i>that's</i> good writing, scary good writing.<br /><br />most of the book concerns the dynamic between this husband and wife. it's a narrow focus on two not incredibly appealing people, which can make it rather hard to read sometimes. but Dick does such a phenomenal job of anatomizing the two, of splitting them into facets and then turning each facet in the light, that it is a weirdly compelling read.<br /><br />which doesn't make it an <i>enjoyable</i> read by any means. i am impressed by Dick's talent and his micro-analytical thoroughness, but i wish he had turned his talents on a more illuminating pair.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11755685928775285266" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13920978213770664732">I knew Little of Philip K .Dick until I started university, but one of the modules on my course in popular culture required us to study Blade Runner, and the book it was based on Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep. I found some of the popular culture modules mind numbing particularly the philosophy of adorno and horkheimer culture industry for example. However one of the bonus’s that came from the course was discovering Phillip as an author, and I have been a fan ever since. This was a book I h</span>
  <span id="freeText13920978213770664732" style="display:none">I knew Little of Philip K .Dick until I started university, but one of the modules on my course in popular culture required us to study Blade Runner, and the book it was based on Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep. I found some of the popular culture modules mind numbing particularly the philosophy of adorno and horkheimer culture industry for example. However one of the bonus’s that came from the course was discovering Phillip as an author, and I have been a fan ever since. This was a book I had my eye on for sometime, and he does not disappoint.<br /><br />Jack Isidore is supposed to be the odd ball, the one that’s mad. The out of quilter black sheep, but the reality is he actually has a very sain rational view of the world compared to the other main protagonists. Charlie and Fay Hume, have some quite disturbing flaws in comparison. The way Phillip K. Dick has written the novel in the first person for multiple characters depending on who is narrating in a particular chapter helps to extenuate this.<br /><br />Personally I found myself cringing and almost feeling physically exasperated at Fay’s motivations and behavior. While in comparison I found Jack’s perspective almost calming and reassuring. It’s also extremely well written, the words just flow of the page into your mind, making it quite a light read. I actually read this in only a matter of days. For that reason it’s probably not one to take on holiday, you would most likely finish before you had even landed.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13920978213770664732" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10107311750892802185">"...it doesn't seem to me that I should be the only person who has to bear the onus of believing an admittedly ridiculous notion. All I want is to see the blame spread around fairly."<br/><br/>And thus we have Confessions of a Crap Artist - the novel that makes transparent all of the little insanities that we conceal so well! Obviously, being a Philip K. Dick novel, it's a little out there - even the most normal character, Nathan Anteil, somehow manages to find himself in love with the most conventionally</span>
  <span id="freeText10107311750892802185" style="display:none">"...it doesn't seem to me that I should be the only person who has to bear the onus of believing an admittedly ridiculous notion. All I want is to see the blame spread around fairly."<br /><br />And thus we have Confessions of a Crap Artist - the novel that makes transparent all of the little insanities that we conceal so well! Obviously, being a Philip K. Dick novel, it's a little out there - even the most normal character, Nathan Anteil, somehow manages to find himself in love with the most conventionally crazy character, Fay. I mean, I get it... but let's just acknowledge that, through Dick's lens, pretty much everyone is nuts, to an exaggerated degree. At least it's humorous.<br /><br />...though it's also disturbing - certainly not on a level with A Scanner Darkly, but there are several starkly violent moments that managed to shake me up a bit. It's a very different side to Dick's writing - still mocking and strange and funny, but also quite dark.<br /><br />It's not Dick's finest. It's a fairly disjointed narrative, and it takes some time to get in to the more interesting parts of the novel - I put it down frequently before finally committing to it - but in the end, it provides a fairly unique commentary. I liked it, but it's difficult to recommend. I'd like to give it a 3.5/5, but lacking the option, I'd go with a 3!</span>

<div id="review_357823184" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/357823184" />
    <a title="Jack Stovold" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8143989-jack-stovold"><img alt="Jack Stovold" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/357823184?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack Stovold" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack Stovold" href="/user/show/8143989-jack-stovold">Jack Stovold</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer357823184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12037334491212768583">My Philip K. Dick Project<br/><br/>Entry #17 - Confessions of a Crap Artist (written Mid 1959, published 1975)<br/><br/>	Confessions of a Crap Artist contains Dick’s most assured and confident writing yet, at turns both bracing and hilarious. After the disappointing Dr. Futurity, a throwback to Dick’s earlier, clunkier style, this book was a joy to read. This is the only “straight", non-sci-fi novel of Dick’s to be published during his lifetime, and it’s not hard to see why. It’s easily the best of the bunch so far</span>
  <span id="freeText12037334491212768583" style="display:none">My Philip K. Dick Project<br /><br />Entry #17 - Confessions of a Crap Artist (written Mid 1959, published 1975)<br /><br />	Confessions of a Crap Artist contains Dick’s most assured and confident writing yet, at turns both bracing and hilarious. After the disappointing Dr. Futurity, a throwback to Dick’s earlier, clunkier style, this book was a joy to read. This is the only “straight", non-sci-fi novel of Dick’s to be published during his lifetime, and it’s not hard to see why. It’s easily the best of the bunch so far. After making a side trip to Idaho in In Milton Lumky Territory, Dick returns to his favorite setting, the San Francisco bay area, this time in a more rural small-town area. The bulk of the action takes place in the house of Fay and Charley Hume.<br />	Fay is the sister of Jack Isidore, the title character, dubbed a “crap artist” by Charley, his brother-in-law. Jack is one of Dick’s best characters, and his chapters are my favorites. Although never explicitly stated by anyone in the book, it’s fairly obvious Charley has some mental problems. He’s clearly obsessive-compulsive. He collects useless junk, like rocks, lengths of string, and the like, and also “crap ideas”. He is an “amateur scientist" who believes the Earth is hollow, that sunlight has weight, and is causing the Earth to become steadily heavier, and most importantly, after falling in with a local group of crackpots, that the world is going to end in about a month, on April 23rd. Jack has no real system of distinguishing fantasy and reality. He believes fervently in anything that’s presented to him interestingly enough.<br />	After being charged with shoplifting by a Seville grocer for stealing chocolate-covered ants (after reading about toads in suspended animation), foul-mouthed Fay and her schlub husband Charley decide to bring him to live in their opulent ranch house in the country, thinking it will be good for him. But it soon becomes evident to Jack that Fay and Charley are as troubled as he. For the first time I can recall, aside from possibly a few of his short stories, Dick writes in the first person, in Jack and Fay’s chapters. For whatever reasons, Charley and Nat’s chapters are written in the third person. Dick has perfected his multiple viewpoints technique, and he plumbs the characters’ minds here as he has never before. In effect, Dick is exploring his favorite theme, the nature of reality here, as the world is vastly different for each of the main characters. In some ways, it reminds one of Eye In The Sky in a more literary way. <br />	At first, Charley seems like little more than a simple-minded wife-beating lummox. But as we see the world from his eyes, we begin to appreciate him as a fully developed, complex human being. The same goes for Fay, initially seeming like a simply a lazy and manipulative housewife, and for Jack, who initially comes off as a useless screwball. In particular, it becomes obvious that Jack for all his eccentricities, is also a decent person, fair, and honest to a fault. His inability to interact with people socially in any way normally propels the action began with Fay’s reckless affair to the book’s most shocking development, the culmination of Charley’s suffering. <br />	Everyone’s a nut. The last chapter, detailing Jack’s epiphany as he calmly awaits the end of the world, is one of Dick’s best passages ever, at the same time humorous and yet sad. Jack may believe in a lot of crap, but everyone around him is as crazy as he is. They hurt the ones they love and they don’t even know why. They lie to each other, and most damningly, they lie to themselves.<br /><br />My edition: Vintage Books, July 1992, paperback<br /><br />Up next: “The Man Whose Teeth Were All Exactly Alike”!<br /><br />July 8th, 2012</span>
  <a data-text-id="12037334491212768583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2917040528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2917040528" />
    <a title="Christian Schwoerke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24022721-christian-schwoerke"><img alt="Christian Schwoerke" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2917040528?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christian Schwoerke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christian Schwoerke" href="/user/show/24022721-christian-schwoerke">Christian Schwoerke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780547572499" href="/book/show/13356656-confessions-of-a-crap-artist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2917040528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1921198129286968725">This is the second of Dick’s non-sf novels that I’ve read, the first being In Milton Lumky Territory, which I read five years ago. I still remember the arc of that novel and some specific details and scenes, which testify to Dick’s ability to practice contemporary literary realism with unexpected skills. The current Confessions is no less interesting for its take on some ordinary aspects of life in the late 1950s (1959, in particular).<br/><br/>Where I quibble with the multiple point-of-view story that Di</span>
  <span id="freeText1921198129286968725" style="display:none">This is the second of Dick’s non-sf novels that I’ve read, the first being In Milton Lumky Territory, which I read five years ago. I still remember the arc of that novel and some specific details and scenes, which testify to Dick’s ability to practice contemporary literary realism with unexpected skills. The current Confessions is no less interesting for its take on some ordinary aspects of life in the late 1950s (1959, in particular).<br /><br />Where I quibble with the multiple point-of-view story that Dick unfolds is how the book asserts in its subtitle that it is the work solely of character Jack Isidore (of Seville, Calif.). While Jack’s story is the first-person narrative that begins and ends the novel—introducing all the players and events of the novel and summing things up at their end—there are chapters in the third person, channeling the points of view of at least three other characters. It’s not a big deal, and I can rationalize the subtitle in at least two ways that do no harm to the story, but it’s a niggling inconsistency…<br /><br />The further clause in the subtitle states of the novel that it is “A Chronicle of Verified Scientific Fact 1945-1959”, which, of course, it’s not. Jack Isidore is an unreliable narrator for many reasons, and he lays them out for us at the beginning and throughout. The “crap” that he ironically refers to is his own interpretation of events, which he infuses with a credulous faith in outré science (including UFOs and beings of superior intelligence that live within the sun). While he is considered loony by all the characters in the novel, it is his final contention that, well, that might be so, but if you look closely, all you other characters aren’t so sane either. Jack’s ruminations about destiny (he was chosen to discern the superior beings’ date for ending life on earth) make him confident, a perfect portrayal of a one succumbing to unverifiable dogma/doctrine. <br /><br />And that’s largely what the novel presents, the complications of personality and circumstance that make actions appear odd and perverse—even crazy—from the outside. Reading this novel as an artifact of the 1950s or early 1960s, it’s easy to suggest that Dick is using the milieu (San Francisco and Marin County) and times to explain his characters’ neurotic and inconsistent behaviors, that it’s these forces that warp natural/sane behavior. Dick’s portrait of Fay nee Isidore Hume, Jack’s adult younger sister is riveting, and she’s the axis around which everything revolves. While Jack and others are willing to call her psychotic, it’s clear that her behavior is more in line with extreme narcissism, where Fay believes that she must be the center of everyone’s attention, once she has deigned to notice them.<br /><br />The unfolding of this story includes two broken marriages, adultery, divorce, heart attack, suicide, and forecast of and reprieve from the earth’s destruction, all centered around a house that’s too expensive to maintain. It’s a credible account of a powerful personality holding sway over the people who come into her orbit, told from the vantage of a brother whose perspective is distorted, but whose vision is no less clear than her victims’.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1921198129286968725" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/872829377?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Holstad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Holstad" href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad">Scott Holstad</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9098678-scott-holstad?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer872829377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13745435107418867833">Okay, okay, I give up! I just can't read Philip K Dick's "mainstream" fiction. It's too awful. Too boring. Too terrible. Too mundane. This book is supposed to be the best of his mainstream novels, and I gave it a chance, I really did. I made it 81 pages into it, but had to give up. Jack Isidore is supposedly fairly crazy, but it's his sister and brother-in-law, Kay and Charley, who are actually nuts. This is a novel about relationships, but there isn't one redeemable character in it. No one you </span>
  <span id="freeText13745435107418867833" style="display:none">Okay, okay, I give up! I just can't read Philip K Dick's "mainstream" fiction. It's too awful. Too boring. Too terrible. Too mundane. This book is supposed to be the best of his mainstream novels, and I gave it a chance, I really did. I made it 81 pages into it, but had to give up. Jack Isidore is supposedly fairly crazy, but it's his sister and brother-in-law, Kay and Charley, who are actually nuts. This is a novel about relationships, but there isn't one redeemable character in it. No one you want to identify with. And it's just drab. Very little actually happens in Dick's mainstream books. They're just so B O R I N G. And to think he's the genius behind dozens of amazing sci fi novels. I know he wanted more than anything to be a mainstream novelist, and for awhile was embarrassed to be a sci fi writer, but God, he should have taken what was given him and left the rest well enough alone. Sci fi is his bread and butter. He just can't write "mainstream." This is the last mainstream novel of his I attempt to read. I own three now and I haven't made it through one. And yet I love his sci fi so much. It's really disappointing. Not recommended, unless you want a good night's sleep.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13745435107418867833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2505758924" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7146083780247659977"> This is a book by Philip K. Dick--but it's not science fiction! So, a little disappointing, but the story is of some interest as it's Dick pointing out--yet again--the absurdity of life. Dick ( born in 1928 in Chicago, IL and died in 1982 in Santa Ana, CA) wrote a series of "mainstream" novels in the 50s that were all rejected. "Confessions of a Crap Artist," written in 1959, was finally published in 1975. Jack Isidore is the "crap artist," so named by his brother-in-law because he's a collecto</span>
  <span id="freeText7146083780247659977" style="display:none"> This is a book by Philip K. Dick--but it's not science fiction! So, a little disappointing, but the story is of some interest as it's Dick pointing out--yet again--the absurdity of life. Dick ( born in 1928 in Chicago, IL and died in 1982 in Santa Ana, CA) wrote a series of "mainstream" novels in the 50s that were all rejected. "Confessions of a Crap Artist," written in 1959, was finally published in 1975. Jack Isidore is the "crap artist," so named by his brother-in-law because he's a collector of "crackpot" ideas, such as his belief in a hollow Earth. Unable to support himself, Jack moves in with his sister's family in their farmhouse in West Marin County in Northern California. He then gets involved with a local cult... But, while Jack seems unconnected to reality, it turns out that his sister Fay and her husband Charley are no less closed off to reality--only in a more acceptable way in their 1950s California suburban environment. I give it *** which may be a little harsh. I really prefer PKD's science fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7146083780247659977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8096713476647673333">A "non-genre" novel by PKD. The only one of several he wrote during the fifties to be published during his life-time - though twenty or so years after it was written. <br/><br/>This was a revelation. I was born in the late sixties so only became 'aware' in the seventies, eighties and beyond... Were there many books like this written in the fifties? Was this the norm? Is this what it was really like in California during that time? This ain't no "American Graffiti" type story. No "Leave it to Beaver" or "Os</span>
  <span id="freeText8096713476647673333" style="display:none">A "non-genre" novel by PKD. The only one of several he wrote during the fifties to be published during his life-time - though twenty or so years after it was written. <br /><br />This was a revelation. I was born in the late sixties so only became 'aware' in the seventies, eighties and beyond... Were there many books like this written in the fifties? Was this the norm? Is this what it was really like in California during that time? This ain't no "American Graffiti" type story. No "Leave it to Beaver" or "Ossie and Harriette" either. It is an oddball family situation that would be a-typical even if set in these crazy modern times.<br /><br />What a weird and wonderful book! Why isn't Quentin Tarantino or the Cohen Brothers making this film?<br /><br />PKD was simply a brilliant writer. And that is all.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2829866488" />
    <a title="Ayushi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30986781-ayushi"><img alt="Ayushi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464935544p2/30986781.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2829866488?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ayushi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ayushi" href="/user/show/30986781-ayushi">Ayushi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2829866488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5238225824513533837">Told from multiple viewpoints, Confessions of a Crap Artist is one of Philip K Dick’s regular fiction books, as opposed to the science fiction novels and short stories that he is famous for. This book has all the typical connotations found in most of his stories, including a protagonist believing bizarre facts and a deceptive and sly female character.<br/><br/>Jack Isidore, the titular Crap Artist of the story, is a socially awkward man living in his own universe and is captivated by weird bits of informa</span>
  <span id="freeText5238225824513533837" style="display:none">Told from multiple viewpoints, Confessions of a Crap Artist is one of Philip K Dick’s regular fiction books, as opposed to the science fiction novels and short stories that he is famous for. This book has all the typical connotations found in most of his stories, including a protagonist believing bizarre facts and a deceptive and sly female character.<br /><br />Jack Isidore, the titular Crap Artist of the story, is a socially awkward man living in his own universe and is captivated by weird bits of information. As he moves in with his sister and her husband, he realizes that the idyllic life of these supposedly sane people is spiraling out of control due to their actions. <br /><br />Replete with Dick’s trademark amusing sense of humor, this book is an examination of sanity and what it means to be normal in this cruel and insane world. As Jack realizes in the end:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>"The whole world is full of nuts. It's enough to get you down."</i>
</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="5238225824513533837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3504882187" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3504882187" />
    <a title="Curtain33" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69949960-curtain33"><img alt="Curtain33" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517964000p2/69949960.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3504882187?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Curtain33" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Curtain33" href="/user/show/69949960-curtain33">Curtain33</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69949960-curtain33?shelf=thought-provoking">thought-provoking</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69949960-curtain33?shelf=engaging">engaging</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69949960-curtain33?shelf=frustrating">frustrating</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3504882187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17508599391896853373">I have a hard time with Philip K. Dick sometimes. I admire him as a person and find him almost infinitely fascinating--in theory I should love all of his work, but I don't. He's an extremely uneven writer and sometimes it feels like he's not interested in the characters, but the idea behind the book. This can make starting some of his works difficult, but this one (unexpectedly) swept me away. I was unaware that PKD had written any mainstream literary fiction until I bought this book. <br/><br/>Confession</span>
  <span id="freeText17508599391896853373" style="display:none">I have a hard time with Philip K. Dick sometimes. I admire him as a person and find him almost infinitely fascinating--in theory I should love all of his work, but I don't. He's an extremely uneven writer and sometimes it feels like he's not interested in the characters, but the idea behind the book. This can make starting some of his works difficult, but this one (unexpectedly) swept me away. I was unaware that PKD had written any mainstream literary fiction until I bought this book. <br /><br />Confessions of a Crap Artist has a confidence to it that many of PKD's other books lack and it has great characters and something to say. I could imagine this book as a Coen brothers movie as I read it: it's violent, funny, painful and perceptive. I keep hearing that this is a great snapshot of the 50's, but I think most of what it has to say is pretty relevant today, because unfortunately socially we haven't come so far. We still laugh at sitcoms that pit a witless husband against a superior wife (read: Everbody Loves Raymond, King of Queens, Home Improvement). What's worst, I know too many people that have relationships that are as shallow and unnuanced as these. Surely we can do better?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17508599391896853373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2911126386" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2911126386" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1868162-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231185226p2/1868162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2911126386?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/1868162-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19904257-confessions-of-a-crap-artist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1868162-nick?shelf=popular-fiction">popular-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1868162-nick?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1868162-nick?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2911126386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7249295794153616423">This is the first of PKD's efforts at mainstreaming his storytelling, that is to say, focusing many of his preoccupations in somewhat naturalistic, non-sci-fi situations. The obsessions remain -- mental illness, the complexities of human relations, conspiracy theories, and greater San Francisco, primarily rural Marin County, CA. This is the story of Faye and Charley -- a domineering wife, kind of a gold-digger, her angry but successful husband, and her slightly nuts brother Jack, the crap artist</span>
  <span id="freeText7249295794153616423" style="display:none">This is the first of PKD's efforts at mainstreaming his storytelling, that is to say, focusing many of his preoccupations in somewhat naturalistic, non-sci-fi situations. The obsessions remain -- mental illness, the complexities of human relations, conspiracy theories, and greater San Francisco, primarily rural Marin County, CA. This is the story of Faye and Charley -- a domineering wife, kind of a gold-digger, her angry but successful husband, and her slightly nuts brother Jack, the crap artist of the title. He moves into their elaborately modern ranch house at about the same time she begins the pursuit of a younger man, whose marriage is wrecked, along with her own. Along the way Jack falls in with a cult group who believe human life is being controlled by superior intelligent aliens with whom they can communicate. Perfect for Jack, who believes he knows when the world will end. This is, by and large, the only sci-fi-ish element of the story. The central figure is Fay, who drives everyone crazy, even as she manipulates everyone into getting whatever she wants. This story reminded me a lot of early Patricia Highsmith, in that we learn to appreciate, even like deeply damaged people as they struggle to control events and each other.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7249295794153616423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1604663944" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1604663944" />
    <a title="Chris Craddock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10581551-chris-craddock"><img alt="Chris Craddock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341352112p2/10581551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1604663944?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Craddock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Craddock" href="/user/show/10581551-chris-craddock">Chris Craddock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10581551-chris-craddock?shelf=beale">beale</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1604663944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1269418642673599486">Hey, Jack Isadore, the titular 'crap artist' belongs to a UFO cult that had him pick the date for the end of the world as April 23. That is in 4 days! But I just wanted to check out what people said & looked on Wikipedia, but there should have been a spoiler alert. Anyway, read 1/2 of book in 1 day. Love it. Should be done either today or tomorrow. NY Primary today. Go Hillary. This is not Science Fiction, but Jack Isadore, is somewhere on the Autistic spectrum, Assberger's Syndrome or close to </span>
  <span id="freeText1269418642673599486" style="display:none">Hey, Jack Isadore, the titular 'crap artist' belongs to a UFO cult that had him pick the date for the end of the world as April 23. That is in 4 days! But I just wanted to check out what people said &amp; looked on Wikipedia, but there should have been a spoiler alert. Anyway, read 1/2 of book in 1 day. Love it. Should be done either today or tomorrow. NY Primary today. Go Hillary. This is not Science Fiction, but Jack Isadore, is somewhere on the Autistic spectrum, Assberger's Syndrome or close to it. His sister Fay is kind of a sociopath, while her husband has OCD and is a wife beater. A fourth character, Nat Antiel, is an intelligent scholar, but is no match for the iron will of Jack's psychopathic sister Fay. She crushes him like an insect. Is his name a pun? Nat = Gnat? Antiel = Ant Hill? Probably. But subtle enough not to elicit any groans. I really liked the Bay Area setting. PKD describes Marin County, Oakland, and San Francisco just as I remembered them. There is even a scene in Children's Fairyland in Oakland that had me tripping down memory lane. In the book there is a UFO Doomsday Cult that expects the world to end on April 23rd. The world did not end, either in the book or in real life. Prince died on April 21st. But the world went on without him. Don't want to give away any Spoilers, but it is an interesting phenomenon, what happens to cult members in most cases where the eschatological predictions fail. Where the world doesn't end. You would think that they would walk away from the cult, but in fact they more likely dig in deeper. Paul Desmond, alto sax player in the Dave Brubeck Group once spotted a former girlfriend who was now dating a banker.<br /><br /> "So this is how the world ends," he said to her. "Not with a whim, but a banker." </span>
  <a data-text-id="1269418642673599486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer355823618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4914175490579252240">Dick is a bit out of his element here. Constrained by a setting which is almost believable, all of Dick's fantastic talents shrivel up and die under the weight of a close approximation of humdrum reality. This is Dick's pathetic attempt to write in someone else's voice: the voice of a successful mainstream writer that he had no hope of becoming.<br/><br/>I am reminded of a story I read about Astor Piazzolla, the brilliant progenitor of Tango Nuevo, who studied under the inestimable tutor of a whole genera</span>
  <span id="freeText4914175490579252240" style="display:none">Dick is a bit out of his element here. Constrained by a setting which is almost believable, all of Dick's fantastic talents shrivel up and die under the weight of a close approximation of humdrum reality. This is Dick's pathetic attempt to write in someone else's voice: the voice of a successful mainstream writer that he had no hope of becoming.<br /><br />I am reminded of a story I read about Astor Piazzolla, the brilliant progenitor of Tango Nuevo, who studied under the inestimable tutor of a whole generation of composers (including Copland and Gardiner), Nadia Boulanger. The story goes that Piazzolla was tooling away at the classical forms he thought he "ought" to write and coming up limp. Boulanger saw his tangos and recognized immediately that his compositional voice lie there, and encouraged him in the pursuit of this less respectable, but more honest expression of Piazzolla's talents and vision.<br /><br />If Dick had had a Boulanger around he might never have written this middling, ineffective novel. But one can hardly blame him for experimentation, considering that his incredible prolificity secured him few material comforts in this world (especially if the rumors of horsemeat are to be believed); let us hope that he has found more and better in the brilliant planes of consciousness he forged and described in the generation-bending novels of paranoid salvation for which he is duly loved.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4914175490579252240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/866164630?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/5033258-jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5033258-jason?shelf=lit-mod">lit-mod</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer866164630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9343789581766154504">It was refreshing to read a Philip Dick novel that did not end in the tiresome alternate reality 'revelation' trope that so often muddles and mires his sci-fi novels. CONFESSIONS OF A CRAP ARTIST is a mainstream novel; the title, however, is disingenuous, because the novel is not a confession and the crap artist is not the center of the story. In fact, Jack Isidore, the 'Crap Artist' (or, one who collects crackpot theories and ideas) could be excised completely from this novel without changing i</span>
  <span id="freeText9343789581766154504" style="display:none">It was refreshing to read a Philip Dick novel that did not end in the tiresome alternate reality 'revelation' trope that so often muddles and mires his sci-fi novels. CONFESSIONS OF A CRAP ARTIST is a mainstream novel; the title, however, is disingenuous, because the novel is not a confession and the crap artist is not the center of the story. In fact, Jack Isidore, the 'Crap Artist' (or, one who collects crackpot theories and ideas) could be excised completely from this novel without changing its tone, plot or impact. The novel should be called ' Scenes from a Crumbling Marriage', because that is what the focus is on. The central characters are Jack's sister, Faye Hume, a strong willed, manipulative modern day Lady MacBeth; Charley Hume, Faye's self-deluded uxorious husband who, when drunk, shamefully realizes his uxoriousness and explodes in scenes of horrific violence; and a young couple, Nate and Gwen, whose friendship suddenly complicates the affairs of the Humes. <br />The plot is simple, dealing with infidelity and its aftermath, but Dick's strong characterizations of Faye and Charley make the novel engrossing and fulfilling. On the basis of CONFESSIONS I will be visiting his other non sci-fi, mainstream novels.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9343789581766154504" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      if (form) {

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4764.Philip_K_Dick">About Philip K. Dick</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4764.Philip_K_Dick"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1264613853p3/4764.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4764.Philip_K_Dick">Philip K. Dick
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
16,984

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10302536908523311681">Philip K. Dick was born in Chicago in 1928 and lived most of his life in California. In 1952, he began writing professionally and proceeded to write numerous novels and short-story collections. He won the Hugo Award for the best novel in 1962 for <i>The Man in the High Castle</i> and the John W. Campbell Memorial Award for best novel of the year in 1974 for <i>Flow My Tears, the Policeman Said</i>. Philip K. Di</span>
  <span id="freeText10302536908523311681" style="display:none">Philip K. Dick was born in Chicago in 1928 and lived most of his life in California. In 1952, he began writing professionally and proceeded to write numerous novels and short-story collections. He won the Hugo Award for the best novel in 1962 for <i>The Man in the High Castle</i> and the John W. Campbell Memorial Award for best novel of the year in 1974 for <i>Flow My Tears, the Policeman Said</i>. Philip K. Dick died on March 2, 1982, in Santa Ana, California, of heart failure following a stroke.<br /><br />In addition to 44 published novels, Dick wrote approximately 121 short stories, most of which appeared in science fiction magazines during his lifetime. Although Dick spent most of his career as a writer in near-poverty, ten of his stories have been adapted into popular films since his death, including <i>Blade Runner, Total Recall, A Scanner Darkly, Minority Report, Paycheck, Next, Screamers</i>, and <i>The Adjustment Bureau</i>. In 2005, Time magazine named <i>Ubik</i> one of the one hundred greatest English-language novels published since 1923. In 2007, Dick became the first science fiction writer to be included in The Library of America series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10302536908523311681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
